option position.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ESettlement price = \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-weight: 400;\"\u003EThe amount paid or received to close the makes FarmLogs' Crop Health images special?","title":"What makes FarmLogs' Crop Health images special?","body":"\u003Cdiv class=\"comment-body markdown\"\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFarmLogs provides both True Color and vegetative imagery for in-season fields, along with vegetative imagery for historical fields. This imagery combination allows you to detect unknown yield threats and observe trends. You can maximize the ability to use Crop Health imagery most effectively and profitably by identifying and defining problems that you can fix before the next season or in some cases during the current season. \u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EIn-season True Color imagery can provide a familiar reference when interpreting satellite imagery. In addition to increasing the value of Wide Dynamic Range Vegetative Indexing (WDRVI), True Color imagery pulls out identifiable features in the field that you can use as a reference. It can also provide you with feedback for natural variability, which can include the following: \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ewater stress\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Enutrient deficiencies \u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Etopography\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Esoil quality\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOur satellite imagery is effortless, as the images simply show up in your account. It's also faster and far less complex than the imagery used by some of our competitors. There is no need to drive to a field, fly a drone over a field, or stitch multiple images together to interpret and act on them. We deliver satellite imagery at 3-5-meter resolution. Some of our competitors offer images at a higher resolution but at less frequent intervals. On average, users can expect around one image per week for each of their fields during the season. \u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003ECloud cover in vegetative imagery sometimes gives the impression that parts of your field have less vegetation than ground truth represents. Before you begin scouting, you can use the True Color imagery to determine if what you're seeing is cloud cover.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C\u002Fdiv\u003E","source_locale":"en-us","locale":"en-us","outdated":false,"outdated_locales":[],"edited_at":"2018-01-18T16:26:17Z","label_names":[],"slug":"understanding-the-features-what-makes-farmlogs-crop-health-images-special"},{"id":115005321746,"url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fapi\u002Fv2\u002Fhelp_center\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F115005321746-What-do-the-colors-in-my-Crop-Health-map-represent-.json","html_url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fhc\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F115005321746-What-do-the-colors-in-my-Crop-Health-map-represent-","author_id":5712763043,"comments_disabled":true,"draft":false,"promoted":false,"position":1,"vote_sum":0,"vote_count":0,"section_id":201074799,"created_at":"2017-10-26T19:24:26Z","updated_at":"2018-01-18T16:33:51Z","name":"What do the colors in my Crop Health map represent?","title":"What do the colors in my Crop Health map represent?","body":"\u003Cp\u003EFarmLogs' in-season crop health maps show variability in crop vegetation for a field at a single point in time. We use a divergent scale (red-yellow-green) to provide a contrast of colors. This makes it easy for you to spot small problem areas and yield performance patterns. Prior to peak vegetation, the darker green areas in the field are healthier than the yellow and red areas. After peak vegetation, the yellow and red indicate the areas of the field that are maturing (i.e., drying down) more quickly than the field overall.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EImportant:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E In an effort to provide as much imagery as possible, some images might contain clouds. Clouds can show up as areas of low vegetation in crop health maps. You can verify whether an area of low vegetation is caused by a cloud by checking the True Color image for that map. Clouds might be highlighted in red or a combination of blue and red in a True Color image.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECrop vigor is measured using Wide Dynamic Range Vegetative Indexing (WDRVI), a vegetative index that is similar to Normalized Difference Vegetative Index (NDVI) but more sensitive to changes in crop biomass. When a satellite passes over a field, it measures the reflectance of both red and near-infrared light (not visible to the human eye) at a 3-5 meter resolution. We then apply a mathematical formula to the observed reflectance values to determine the WDRVI value for every 5 square meters of the field. After we determine the WDRVI value, we use it to color each 5-meter block, where darker green means a higher value.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA map's colors are scaled relative to the WDRVI values observed in each individual image. These colors represent the range of vegetation in a field when the image was taken. Specifically, the darkest green in an image represents the highest vegetation value in that image. Similarly, the darkest red in an image represents the lowest vegetation value in that image. However, these colors do not represent the same vegetation values in other images, which means that making comparisons between images can be difficult.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETo better understand the range of vegetation values in an image, we recommend that you reference the legend. The colored region of the legend shows the range of WDRVI values in an image. This allows you to see how WDRVI values compare across images throughout the growing season.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E","source_locale":"en-us","locale":"en-us","outdated":false,"outdated_locales":[],"edited_at":"2018-01-18T16:33:51Z","label_names":[],"slug":"understanding-the-features-what-do-the-colors-in-my-crop-health-map-represent"},{"id":115005321566,"url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fapi\u002Fv2\u002Fhelp_center\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F115005321566-When-should-I-receive-Crop-Health-images-.json","html_url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fhc\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F115005321566-When-should-I-receive-Crop-Health-images-","author_id":5712763043,"comments_disabled":true,"draft":false,"promoted":false,"position":2,"vote_sum":1,"vote_count":1,"section_id":201074799,"created_at":"2017-10-26T19:18:47Z","updated_at":"2018-02-20T15:41:05Z","name":"When should I receive Crop Health images? The NOAA sources the data from multiple radar and ground stations to algorithmically calculate the amount of precipitation that falls on a high-resolution grid across the continental United States. The NOAA factors in variables like wind and terrain that influence where the rain actually hits the ground. This is done within 0.62 miles (1 km) of accuracy. The rainfall grid data is gauge-corrected every hour, after which we check for updates and match them up with your field boundaries.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe following information describes what the different rainfall totals in FarmLogs represent:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe rectangle next to a field indicates the approximate amount of rain that a field received in a recent rainfall event. To the right of the rectangle is when that recent rainfall event was. If there is no rectangle next to a field, then there has not been a recent rainfall event.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe daily rainfall charts indicate the amount of rainfall received on a field between 12:00 AM and 11:59 PM (your local time) on that day.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EThe cumulative rainfall charts indicate the total amount of rainfall received on a field across multiple dates.\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIf you log a planting activity to a field, the rainfall amounts will be specific to the growing season rather than the calendar year.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENote:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E This feature is currently only available in the continental United States, Canada, and Mexico.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E","source_locale":"en-us","locale":"en-us","outdated":false,"outdated_locales":[],"edited_at":"2018-01-18T16:28:19Z","label_names":[],"slug":"understanding-the-features-how-the-rainfall-feature-works"},{"id":204654499,"url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fapi\u002Fv2\u002Fhelp_center\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F204654499-How-FarmLogs-calculates-growth-stage-and-GDD.json","html_url":"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.zendesk.com\u002Fhc\u002Fen-us\u002Farticles\u002F204654499-How-FarmLogs-calculates-growth-stage-and-GDD","author_id":402078155,"comments_disabled":true,"draft":false,"promoted":false,"position":4,"vote_sum":237,"vote_count":237,"section_id":201074799,"created_at":"2015-06-17T22:32:06Z","updated_at":"2018-01-18T16:28:04Z","name":"How FarmLogs calculates growth stage and GDD","title":"How FarmLogs calculates growth stage and GDD","body":"\u003Cp\u003EFarmLogs uses the USDA's growth stage model for corn, which is based on Growing Degree Days (GDD). This is an 86\u002F50-bounded calculation, which only accumulates when temperatures are optimal for corn growth. AAR uses cues such as the amount of time that you are in a field's boundaries and the distance and pattern that you drive in the field to determine whether an activity is being performed. After an activity has been detected, a notification is sent to your mobile device. The details of the activity can be added then or at a later time. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYou can add users to your account so they can use AAR on their mobile devices to track activities for your fields. When it comes to developing a strategy there's no one answer that works for everyone. Use this page as a resource for learning more about basic marketing terms, and don't forget to consult your advisor when determining which strategies are best for your farm. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fwebinar-intro-to-grain-marketing\"\u003EWatch: Introduction to Grain Marketing »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fquick-tips-for-successful-grain-marketing\"\u003ERead: Quick Tips for Successful Grain Marketing »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EUnderstanding your cost of production \u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe first step towards creating an effective marketing plan is understanding your cost of production. Having a complete understanding of your production costs per acre helps you make better decisions about when to sell your crop.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fsee-your-states-average-cost-of-production-to-create-your-2018-budget\"\u003EFind your state's average cost of production »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003ECalculating your break-even price\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EKnowing your cost of production helps you calculate your break-even price, the price you need to sell your grain at to cover the costs of growing it. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fknow-your-costs\"\u003ERead: Know Your Costs »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EHow are other farmers marketing their crops?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe surveyed over 1,000 U.S. growers to uncover trends in how U.S. farms plan, strategize, and make grain marketing decisions. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fgrowing_18\u002Fstate-of-grain_18\u002F2018%20State%20of%20Grain%20Report.pdf\"\u003ERead: 2018 State of Grain Marketing Report »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E","direction":"left"}},{"component":"TextSection","props":{"id":"diversifying","text":"\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EDiversifying &amp; Hedging\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003EGrowing your crop efficiently and producing great yields is only part of what it takes to keep your farm in business. The other part is making sure you sell your crops at a price that covers your costs and makes a profit. Diversifying your crop marketing plan is similar to having crop insurance: you want to protect the investment of capital, time, and hard work your put into growing your crop.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EWhat we can learn from historical corn prices \u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen looking at corn price trends year-over-year, we can see that prices are historically higher during the growing season and lower during harvest. It's estimated that two-thirds of farmers are selling in the bottom one-third of the market, meaning those selling their grain during harvest aren't taking advantage of higher prices available at other times in the year. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fcorn-price-trends-over-time\"\u003ERead: Corn Prices Over Time »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EHedging\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EHedging is a great way to mitigate your risk of not selling at a price that's profitable to your operation. The idea behind hedging is that the cash market prices and futures market prices typically move up and down together, so you can lessen your risk of a loss in the cash market by taking an opposite position in the futures market. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fwhy-hedge\"\u003ERead: Why Hedge »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fgrain-marketing-strategies-decision-guide\"\u003ERead: Grain Marketing Strategies Decision Guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EStaying up-to-date with the market\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAnother important piece of developing a diversified marketing plan is staying up-to-date with the market. There are a lot of market newsletters you can subscribe to, and don't forget, you can always consult with an advisor for more personalized insights!\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fadvisor-consultation-interest-form\"\u003ESchedule a FREE consultation with a FarmLogs Advisor Partner »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EWhat are other farmers doing?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDave S. is a 52 year old farmer who helps run his family's grain and livestock operation in Wisconsin. In this article, he addresses the challenges of successfully marketing a crop, and why diversifying his marketing plan was key to running a more profitable farm. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Ffrom-our-farmers-diversifying-your-grain-marketing-plan\"\u003ERead: Diversifying Your Grain Marketing Plan »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E","direction":"left"}},{"component":"TextSection","props":{"id":"futures","text":"\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnderstanding Futures Contracts \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA futures contract is an agreement to buy or sell a specific amount of a commodity at a set price. Futures contracts are created by and traded on the exchange. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EFutures contracts are settled by an offsetting transaction or by delivery of the commodity. Delivery is uncommon, and accounts for approximately 2% of all traded agricultural commodities. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EFutures contracts are sometimes referred to as \"paper contracts\" because the physical commodity isn't actually traded on the exchange. Rather, paper contracts representing those commodities are traded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fwebinar-recording-futures-options-and-strategizing-in-todays-market\"\u003EWatch: Understanding Futures, Options, and Strategizing »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fusing-futures-hta-forward-contracts\"\u003EWatch: When to use futures, HTA, and forward contracts »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EHow is my cash price determined?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe final price you get for your commodity, or the cash price, is a combination of the futures price plus basis. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EHow is the futures price determined?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe futures price is set on an exchange and determined by price discovery (global supply and demand).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EHow is basis determined?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBasis is the difference between the cash and futures price. Transportation costs, commodity availability, and other factors contribute to basis, which is why the price you see on the exchange is different from your local cash price. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA basis is \"strengthened\" when it improves the price you get for a commodity. A basis is \"weakened\" when it worsens the price you get for a commodity. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EFutures contract codes\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Cp\u003EFutures contracts are references by codes that indicate the commodity and the month and year of the contract's expiration. Below, you'll see a list of contract month codes: \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EJanuary = F\u003Cbr\u003EFebruary = G\u003Cbr\u003EMarch = H\u003Cbr\u003EApril = J\u003Cbr\u003EMay = K\u003Cbr\u003EJune = M\u003Cbr\u003EJuly = N\u003Cbr\u003EAugust = Q\u003Cbr\u003ESeptember = U\u003Cbr\u003EOctober = V\u003Cbr\u003ENovember = X\u003Cbr\u003EDecember = Z\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EExample: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EA corn futures contract that expires in December of 2019 would be CZ9.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EWhat is margin? \u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you're buying or selling futures on an exchange through a broker, you'll be required to deposit money with your broker. While there are minimum levels that the exchange has established, your broker's firm will determine the amount of money needed for deposit. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMargins are used to ensure the performance of your contract. If you have a short position and changes in the market causes the futures price to increase, you may be required to deposit more money, which is referred to as \"margin call\". \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003ESelling Futures\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen you sell a futures contract (also called a \"short futures\" contract), you are agreeing to sell a set quantity of a commodity on a set date for a set price (keep in mind, these contracts are typically not held to expiration and therefore do not have a delivery obligation).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen you're ready to deliver your physical crop on the cash market to your local elevator, you would settle your futures position through an offsetting transaction (in this case, buying the same quantity of futures contracts as what you sold to open the position). This is referred to as \"lifting your hedge\".\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESelling a futures contract allows you to essentially \"lock in\" the price you'll net for a percentage of your crop come harvest by offsetting a potential loss for a decline in the cash market price. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003ENote: This process only pertains to selling futures through a broker on the exchange, not through an elevator on the cash market. \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ffarmlogs.com\u002Feducation#cash\"\u003EGo to the Cash Market Sales &amp; Hedges section to learn more »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EExample: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EIt's June and you're a corn farmer with your crop in the ground. Right now, the cash price for corn delivered in November is $4.00\u002Fbushel. Hedging a portion of your bushels by selling a futures contract would help you offset the risk of a cash price decline during harvest, as a decrease in the cash price results in a gain on your futures position. To keep this example simple, we're assuming a basis of $0.00.\u003Cbr\u003E  \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EJune  (Dec Corn Futures at $4.00)   \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = sell cash Corn at $4.00\u002Fbu\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = sell Dec Corn Futures at $4.00\u002Fbu\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENovember (Dec Corn Futures at $3.00)   \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = sell cash Corn at $3.00\u002Fbu\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = buy Dec Corn Futures at 3.00\u002Fbu\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGain \u002F Loss\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = $1.00 loss\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = $1.00 gain\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENet Sale Price\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E$3.00 (from selling cash corn)  + $1.00 (from gain on futures) = $4.00\u002Fbushel\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E  \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EEven though you had a loss in the cash market, you offset that loss through your short hedge, making your net sale price $4.00\u002Fbushel. If prices increased by $1.00, you would still end up with a net sale price of $4.00\u002Fbushel.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EJune (Dec Corn Futures at $4.00)       \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = sell cash Corn at $4.00\u002Fbu\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = sell Dec Corn Futures at $4.00\u002Fbu\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENovember (Dec Corn Futures at $5.00)   \u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = sell cash Corn at $5.00\u002Fbu\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = buy Dec Corn Futures at 5.00\u002Fbu\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EGain \u002F Loss\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003ECash Market = $1.00 gain\u003Cbr\u003EFutures Market = $1.00 loss\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ENet Sale Price\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E$5.00 (from selling cash corn)  – $1.00 (from loss on futures) = $4.00\u002Fbushel\u003C\u002Fem\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe main idea behind a short futures contract? Offset the risk of falling prices on the cash market by taking an equal but opposite position in the futures market. With this strategy, you'll have a better idea of the net price you'll get for your grain and can more easily make decisions about in-season expenses. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fcontract%20examples\u002Fshort-futures_contract-example.pdf\"\u003ERead: Short Futures Example Guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EBuying Futures\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBuying a futures contract is simply the opposite of selling a futures contract. When you buy a futures contract (also called a \"long futures\" contract) you are agreeing to buy a set quantity of a commodity on a set date for a fixed price if you hold the contract to expiration. Keep in mind, these contracts are typically not held to expiration and therefore do not have a delivery obligation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBuying a futures contract increases your exposure to a commodity market. If the market moves higher, your long position will gain in value. If the market moves lower, your position will lose value. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E","direction":"left"}},{"component":"TextSection","props":{"id":"options","text":"\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnderstanding Options\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAn option is a contract with an expiration date that gives the contract holder the right, but not the obligation, to buy or sell a futures contract of the underlying commodity at a specific price. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThere are two types of options: calls and puts. Call options give you the right to buy a futures contract of the underlying commodity. Put options give you the right to sell a futures contract of the underlying commodity. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EKeep in mind, both calls and puts have buyers and sellers. Buyers are buying the right of an option, and sellers are selling the right. Option buyers must pay a premium to the option sellers to buy the option contract. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe difference between futures and options is the difference between \"have to\" versus \"can\". If you've entered into a futures contract, you \u003Cem\u003Ehave to\u003C\u002Fem\u003E sell or buy the underlying commodity for the set date and price. If you've entered into an option contract, you \u003Cem\u003Ecan \u003C\u002Fem\u003Esell or buy the commodity for the set price. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblog.farmlogs.com\u002Fusing-puts-and-calls\"\u003EWatch: When to Use Options (Puts and Calls) »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EStrike price and premiums\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe strike price (or exercise price) on an option is the price at which the option holder can buy or sell an underlying futures contract. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe option premium is what the buyer of the option pays and the seller receives to enter into the option contract.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EHow are premiums determined?\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EJust like the price of the underlying commodity, option premiums are determined by buyers and sellers on the exchange. Time until expiration, the level of the strike price relative to the market, and the volatility of the underlying commodity are some of the main factors that contribute to premiums.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe premium of an option is determined by two of factors: the intrinsic value and the time. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe intrinsic value is how much the option is worth if it expired today. If the option has intrinsic value, then it is said to be \"in the money\". If the option doesn't have intrinsic value, it is said to be \"out of the money\". If the strike price is equal to the market price, the option is said to be \"at the money\". \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETime value is determined by subtracting the intrinsic value from the option premium, and it also factors in risk. The time value of an option is greater when: \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\u003Cli\u003Eyou are further from expiration\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003Cli\u003Ethe strike price is closer to the market price\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EBuying a call (long call)\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBuying a call gives you the right, but not the obligation, to buy a commodity at a predetermined strike price. If the market goes above your strike price, you are \"in the money\" and will profit from the difference between the current futures price and the strike price, minus the premium you paid for the call. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the market goes below your strike price, you are \"out of the money\", and your call would expire worthless. Your loss is limited to the premium you paid for the call.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EExample: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EYou are a soybean farmer and suspect that prices will go up, but want to sell your crop now to meet your cash flow needs. You buy a call, giving you the right to \"re-own\" your crop at today's market price. If the market rallies, you can buy beans at today's price and then sell them at the new, higher price. If the market stays flat or falls, you would not exercise the option, and end up with the price you sold your beans for on the cash market. In both instances, you lose the premium you paid to buy the call. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fgrowing_18\u002Fgrowing_18_contract-examples\u002Fcontract-ex_long-call.pdf\"\u003ERead: Long Call Example Guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003ESelling a call (short call)\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhen you sell a call, you are selling someone the right, but not the obligation, to buy a commodity from you. You collect a premium in exchange for capping your upside potential in a market rally. This is because when the market moves above the strike price the call you sold is \"in the money\" and in all likelihood will be exercised by the buyer of that call. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the option is exercised, the strike price plus the premium you collect makes up the final price you receive for your crop.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EBuying a put (long put)\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA put option gives you the right, but not the obligation, to sell a commodity at a predetermined strike price. If the market goes above your strike price, you are \"out of the money\" and your put would expire, worthless. You are free to market your crop at the current higher price, and your loss is limited to the premium you paid for the put. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the market goes below your strike price, you are \"in the money\" and would exercise your option, selling your commodity at the higher strike price. Your loss is limited to the premium you paid.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EExample: \u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EYou are a corn farmer who likes the current market levels and wants to take risk off the table while leaving yourself open to participate in upside if their is a market rally. You buy a put, giving you the right to sell your crop at the strike price. If the market rallies, you let the option expire and are free to sell your corn at the market price. If the market falls, you would exercise your put and sell your corn at the strike price. In both instances, you lose the premium you paid to buy the call. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fgrowing_18\u002Fgrowing_18_contract-examples\u002Fcontract-ex_long-put.pdf\"\u003ERead: Long Put Example Guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EExiting an option position\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThere are three ways to exit an option position: offset, exercise, and expiration. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYou can offset your option position by taking an equal yet opposite position. \u003Cstrong\u003EExample:\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E If you sold a call, you would buy a call to close the position and be neutral to the market.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the option has value at expiration, it will be exercised. If it does not, it will simply expire, and the only loss you'll incur will be the price of the option's premium.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf you are the holder of an option and choose to exercise, both you (the buyer) and the seller of that option will be assigned with a corresponding futures contract at the strike price. The only time a buyer would exercise an option is when it has intrinsic value (in the money). For a call, that means the market has moved above the strike price. For a put, that means the market has moved below the strike price. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E","direction":"left"}},{"component":"TextSection","props":{"id":"cash","text":"\u003Ch2\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnderstanding Cash Sales &amp; Hedges\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fh2\u003E\u003Cp\u003ECash contracts are used on the cash market (also called the physical market) and ultimately require physical delivery of a commodity. While these contracts won't require the seller to put up margin, there is often  a per bushel fee charged by the buyer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EForward cash sales\u002FCash contracts\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA forward cash sale, or cash contract, is an agreement to sell a specific amount of grain for a fixed price to a buyer in the cash market, delivered on a certain date in the future. Both the futures price and the basis are locked.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fgrowing_18\u002Fgrowing_18_contract-examples\u002Fcash-sale_contract-example.pdf\"\u003ERead: Cash Sale Example Guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003EFutures only (FO)\u002FHedge-to-arrive (HTA) contracts\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA futures only (or hedge-to-arrive) contract allows you to lock in the futures price without having to set the basis. Before the contract expires, you will be required to set a basis. Some contracts also allow for flexible delivery, in which case you will also need to set the delivery location. There is usually a per bushel fee charged by the buyer (typically 2–3 cents) in exchange for using their hedge account. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYour final price will be the futures price determined in the contract plus or minus your basis upon delivery and any fees charged by the buyer.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EMin\u002Fmax contracts\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMin\u002Fmax contracts allow you to set a ceiling and floor for the futures price, essentially predetermining a range for the futures price. Typically basis is not set at the time you enter into the contract, but is set at a later date. There is usually a per bushel fee charged by the buyer of around 2–3 cents. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIf the market finishes above your ceiling, your final price will be the ceiling you set. If the market finishes below your floor, your final price will be the floor you set. If the market finishes in between your floor and ceiling, your final price will be the current market price. In each scenario, your final price will be adjusted by the fees charged by the buyer.  \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.farmlogs.com\u002Fhubfs\u002Fgrowing_18\u002Fgrowing_18_contract-examples\u002Fhta_contract-example.pdf\"\u003ERead: Hedge-to-arrive example guide »\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EAverager\u002Fequalizer contracts\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAverager (or equalizer) contracts allow you to price equal percentages of your crop at set intervals throughout a predetermined window of time. These contracts hedge the futures component of your cash price so you are free to manage basis separately. Basis will be set on or before delivery. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYour final price is an average of each sale made throughout the predetermined window, plus or minus the basis and any fees charged by the buyer. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003Cbr\u003EBasis contracts\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBasis contracts allow you to lock in basis without having to set the futures price. The elevator will give you a deadline to set your futures price, at which point your basis contract will be converted to a cash contract. \u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EYour final price will be the futures price determined in the contract plus or minus your basis and any fees charged by the buyer.
